0.4em">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_modelling" title="Rail transport modelling">Modelling</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Train_topics" title="Template:Train topics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Train_topics" title="Template talk:Train topics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Train_topics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>railway system in Great Britain</b> is the oldest in the world. The first locomotive-hauled public railway opened in 1825, which was followed by an era of rapid expansion. Whilst the network suffered gradual attrition from about 1900 onwards, and a severe rationalisation in the mid-1960s, the network is again in a position of growth.\n</p><p>Most of the railway track is managed by <a href="/wiki/Network_Rail" title="Network Rail">Network Rail</a>, which in 2017 had a network of 15,811 kilometres (9,824&#160;mi) of <a href="/wiki/Standard-gauge" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard-gauge">standard-gauge</a> lines, of which 5,374 kilometres (3,339&#160;mi) were <a href="/wiki/Railway_electrification_in_Great_Britain" title="Railway electrification in Great Britain">electrified</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ORR-infra-2016-17_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ORR-infra-2016-17-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ORR-table-2.52_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ORR-table-2.52-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> These lines range from <a href="/wiki/Single_track_(rail)" class="mw-redirect" title="Single track (rail)">single</a> to <a href="/wiki/Quadruple_track" title="Quadruple track">quadruple track</a> or more. In addition, some cities have separate rail-based mass transit systems (including the extensive and historic <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a>). There are also several <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_heritage_and_private_railways" title="List of British heritage and private railways">private railways</a> (some of them <a href="/wiki/British_narrow_gauge_railways" class="mw-redirect" title="British narrow gauge railways">narrow-gauge</a>), which are primarily short tourist lines. The British railway network is connected with that of <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a> by an undersea rail link, the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Tunnel" title="Channel Tunnel">Channel Tunnel</a>, opened in 1994.\n</p><p>The United Kingdom is a member of the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Railways" title="International Union of Railways">International Union of Railways</a> (UIC). The <a href="/wiki/UIC_Country_Code" class="mw-redirect" title="UIC Country Code">UIC Country Code</a> for United Kingdom is 70. The UK has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_rail_transport_network_size" title="List of countries by rail transport network size">17th largest railway network in the world</a>; despite many lines having closed in the 20th century it remains one of the densest rail networks. It is <a href="/wiki/Rail_usage_statistics_by_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Rail usage statistics by country">one of the busiest railways</a> in Europe, with 20% more train services than France, 60% more than Italy, and more than Spain, Switzerland, The Netherlands, Portugal and Norway combined, as well as representing more than 20% of all passenger journeys in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016, there were 1.718 billion journeys on the <a href="/wiki/National_Rail" title="National Rail">National Rail</a> network,<sup id="cite_ref-2016usage_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2016usage-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> making the British network the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_rail_usage" title="List of countries by rail usage">fifth most used in the world</a> (Great Britain ranks 23rd in world population). Unlike a number of other countries, rail travel in the United Kingdom has enjoyed a renaissance in recent years, with passenger numbers reaching their highest ever level (see usage figures below). This has coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Privatisation_of_British_Rail" title="Privatisation of British Rail">privatisation of British Rail</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/Impact_of_the_privatisation_of_British_Rail" title="Impact of the privatisation of British Rail">effect of this is disputed</a>. The growth is partly attributed to a shift away from private motoring due to growing road congestion and increasing petrol prices, but also to the overall increase in travel due to affluence.<sup id="cite_ref-rail20110810p22_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rail20110810p22-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> However, passenger journeys in the UK grew by 88% over the period 1997-98 to 2014 as compared to 62% in Germany, 41% in France and 16% in Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>To cope with increasing passenger numbers, there is a large ongoing <a href="/wiki/History_of_rail_transport_in_Great_Britain_1995_to_date#Timeline_of_improvements" title="History of rail transport in Great Britain 1995 to date">programme of upgrades</a> to the network, including <a href="/wiki/Thameslink_Programme" title="Thameslink Programme">Thameslink</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crossrail" title="Crossrail">Crossrail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Railway_electrification_in_Great_Britain" title="Railway electrification in Great Britain">electrification of lines</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Rail_Traffic_Management_System_in_Great_Britain" title="European Rail Traffic Management System in Great Britain">in-cab signalling</a>, new <a href="/wiki/Intercity_Express_Programme" title="Intercity Express Programme">inter-city trains</a> and a new <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_2" title="High Speed 2">high-speed line</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Historical_overview"><span services</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Annual_passenger_numbers"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Annual passenger numbers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Railway_stations"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Railway stations</span></a></li>\n<li 3</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-12"><a href="#High-speed_rolling_stock"><span class="tocnumber">2.3.5</span> <span class="toctext">High-speed rolling stock</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Local_metro_and_other_rail_systems"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Local metro and other rail systems</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Goods_services"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Goods services</span></a></li>\n<li <span class="toctext">Leasing companies (ROSCO)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Spot-hire_companies"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Spot-hire companies</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Statutory_framework"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Statutory framework</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Railway_industry"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Railway industry</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Statutory_authorities"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Statutory authorities</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Rail_network_and_signalling_operations"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Rail network and signalling operations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Other_national_entities"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Other national entities</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-26"><a href="#Trade_unions"><span class="tocnumber">6.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Trade unions</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Regional_entities"><span class="tocnumber">6.4</span> <span class="toctext">Regional entities</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Freight_railway_companies"><span class="tocnumber">6.5</span> <span class="toctext">Freight railway companies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Open_access_operators_and_other_non-franchised_passenger_operators"><span class="tocnumber">6.6</span> <span class="toctext">Open access operators and other non-franchised passenger operators</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Early_railway_companies_(1820s-1840s)"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Early railway companies (1820s-1840s)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Heritage_and_private_railways"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Heritage and private railways</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#Railway_re-opening"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Railway re-opening</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Rail_link(s)_with_adjacent_countries"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Rail link(s) with adjacent countries</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Rail-ferry-rail_services"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Rail-ferry-rail services</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_overview">Historical overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Historical overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UK_railway_map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/UK_railway_map.png/350px-UK_railway_map.png" width="350" height="340" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/UK_railway_map.png/525px-UK_railway_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/UK_railway_map.png/700px-UK_railway_map.png 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="700" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UK_railway_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Current mainline railway lines in <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> are shown in black, heritage lines in green, subway lines in red and former railway lines in light blue.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GBR_rail_passengers_by_year_1830-2015.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/GBR_rail_passengers_by_year_1830-2015.png/360px-GBR_rail_passengers_by_year_1830-2015.png" width="360" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/GBR_rail_passengers_by_year_1830-2015.png/540px-GBR_rail_passengers_by_year_1830-2015.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/GBR_rail_passengers_by_year_1830-2015.png/720px-GBR_rail_passengers_by_year_1830-2015.png 2x" data-file-width="1079" data-file-height="649" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GBR_rail_passengers_by_year_1830-2015.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rail Passengers in Great Britain from 1829-2016, showing the early era of small railway companies, the amalgamation into the "Big Four", nationalisation and finally the current era of privatisation</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_rail_transport_in_Great_Britain" title="History of rail transport in Great Britain">History of rail transport in Great Britain</a></div>\n<p>The railways started with the building of local isolated wooden wagonways in 1560s. These wagonways soon spread out across the country. The system was later built as patchwork of local lines operated by small private railway companies. Over the course of the 19th and early 20th centuries, these amalgamated or were bought by competitors until only a handful of larger companies remained (see <a href="/wiki/Railway_Mania" title="Railway Mania">Railway Mania</a>). The entire network was brought under government control during the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a> and a number of advantages of amalgamation and planning were revealed. However, the government resisted calls for the <a href="/wiki/Nationalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalisation">nationalisation</a> of the network (first proposed by 19th century <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a> as early as the 1830s). Instead, from 1 January 1923, almost all the remaining companies were <a href="/wiki/Railways_Act_1921" title="Railways Act 1921">grouped</a> into the "big four": the <a href="/wiki/Great_Western_Railway" title="Great Western Railway">Great Western Railway</a>, the <a href="/wiki/London_and_North_Eastern_Railway" title="London and North Eastern Railway">London and North Eastern Railway</a>, the <a href="/wiki/London,_Midland_and_Scottish_Railway" title="London, Midland and Scottish Railway">London, Midland and Scottish Railway</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Railway_(UK)" title="Southern Railway (UK)">Southern Railway</a> companies (there were also a number of other <a href="/wiki/Joint_railway" title="Joint railway">joint railways</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Midland_and_Great_Northern_Joint_Railway" title="Midland and Great Northern Joint Railway">Midland and Great Northern Joint Railway</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cheshire_Lines_Committee" title="Cheshire Lines Committee">Cheshire Lines Committee</a> as well as special joint railways such as the <a href="/wiki/Forth_Bridge" title="Forth Bridge">Forth Bridge</a> Railway, <a href="/wiki/Ryde_Pier" title="Ryde Pier">Ryde Pier</a> Railway and at one time the <a href="/wiki/East_London_Railway" class="mw-redirect" title="East London Railway">East London Railway</a>). The "Big Four" were joint-stock public companies and they continued to run the railway system until 31 December 1947.\n</p><p>The growth in road transport during the 1920s and 1930s greatly reduced revenue for the rail companies. Rail companies accused the government of favouring road haulage through the subsidised construction of roads. The railways entered a slow decline owing to a lack of investment and changes in transport policy and lifestyles. During the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> the companies\' managements joined together, effectively forming one company. A maintenance backlog developed during the war and the private sector only had two years to deal with this after the war ended. After 1945, for both practical and ideological reasons, the government decided to bring the rail service into the <a href="/wiki/Public_sector" title="Public sector">public sector</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationalisation">Nationalisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Nationalisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_rail_transport_in_Great_Britain_1948_-_1994" class="mw-redirect" title="History of rail transport in Great Britain 1948 - 1994">History of rail transport in Great Britain 1948 - 1994</a></div>\n<p>From the start of 1948, the "big four" were <a href="/wiki/Nationalised" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalised">nationalised</a> to form <a href="/wiki/British_Railways" class="mw-redirect" title="British Railways">British Railways</a> (latterly "British Rail") under the control of the <a href="/wiki/British_Transport_Commission" title="British Transport Commission">British Transport Commission</a>. Although BR was a single entity, it was divided into six (later five) regional authorities in accordance with the existing areas of operation. Though there were few initial changes to the service, usage increased and the network became profitable. Regeneration of track and railway stations was completed by 1954. In the same year, changes to the British Transport Commission, including the privatisation of road haulage, ended the coordination of transport in Great Britain. Rail revenue fell and in 1955 the network again ceased to be profitable. The mid-1950s saw the rapid introduction of diesel and electric rolling stock, but the expected transfer back from road to rail did not occur and losses began to mount.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beeching2_clear.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Beeching2_clear.svg/220px-Beeching2_clear.svg.png" width="220" height="362" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Beeching2_clear.svg/330px-Beeching2_clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Beeching2_clear.svg/440px-Beeching2_clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1480" data-file-height="2436" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beeching2_clear.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The national network might have looked like this by the 1980s if the lines not proposed for development in Beeching II had closed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (July 2016)">original research?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The desire for profitability led to a major reduction in the network during the mid-1960s, with <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chemical_Industries" title="Imperial Chemical Industries">ICI</a> manager Dr. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Beeching" title="Richard Beeching">Richard Beeching</a> commissioned by the government under <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Marples" title="Ernest Marples">Ernest Marples</a> with reorganising the railways. Many branch lines (and a number of main lines) were closed because they were deemed uneconomic ("the <a href="/wiki/Beeching_Axe" class="mw-redirect" title="Beeching Axe">Beeching Axe</a>" of 1963), removing much feeder traffic from main line passenger services. In the second Beeching report of 1965, only the "major trunk routes" were selected for large-scale investment, leading many to speculate the rest of the network would eventually be closed. This was never implemented by BR.\n</p><p>Passenger services experienced a renaissance with the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/InterCity_125" title="InterCity 125">InterCity 125</a> trains in the 1970s. Passenger levels fluctuated since then, increasing during periods of economic growth and falling during recessions. The 1980s saw severe cuts in government funding and above-<a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> increases in fares,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and the service became more cost-effective<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. In the early 1990s, the five geographical Regions were replaced by a Sectored organisation, in which passenger services were organised into <a href="/wiki/InterCity_(British_Rail)" title="InterCity (British Rail)">InterCity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Network_SouthEast" title="Network SouthEast">Network SouthEast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Regional_Railways" title="Regional Railways">Regional Railways</a> sectors.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Privatisation">Privatisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Privatisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Privatisation_of_British_Rail" title="Privatisation of British Rail">Privatisation of British Rail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Impact_of_the_privatisation_of_British_Rail" title="Impact of the privatisation of British Rail">Impact of the privatisation of British Rail</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_rail_transport_in_Great_Britain_1995_to_date" title="History of rail transport in Great Britain 1995 to date">History of rail transport in Great Britain 1995 to date</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Privatisation_of_British_Rail" title="Privatisation of British Rail">British Rail operations were privatised</a> during 1994\xe2\x80\x931997. Ownership of the track and infrastructure passed to <a href="/wiki/Railtrack" title="Railtrack">Railtrack</a>, whilst passenger operations were franchised to individual private sector operators (originally there were 25 franchises) and the goods services sold outright (six companies were set up, but five of these were sold to the same buyer). The government said privatisation would see an improvement in passenger services and satisfaction (according to the National Rail Passenger survey) has indeed gone up from 76% in 1999 (when the survey started) to 83% in 2013 and the number of passengers not satisfied with their journey dropped from 10% to 6%.<sup id="cite_ref-gbraildataset19978_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gbraildataset19978-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Since privatisation, passenger levels have more than doubled, and have surpassed their level in the late 1940s. Train fares cost 2.7% more than under British Rail in real terms on average.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> However, while the price of anytime and off-peak tickets has increased, the price of Advance tickets has dramatically decreased in real terms: the average Advance ticket in 1995 cost \xc2\xa39.14 (in 2014 prices) compared to \xc2\xa35.17 in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Subsidy_per_passenger_journey_for_UK,_Germany_and_France.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Subsidy_per_passenger_journey_for_UK%2C_Germany_and_France.png/400px-Subsidy_per_passenger_journey_for_UK%2C_Germany_and_France.png" width="400" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Subsidy_per_passenger_journey_for_UK%2C_Germany_and_France.png/600px-Subsidy_per_passenger_journey_for_UK%2C_Germany_and_France.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Subsidy_per_passenger_journey_for_UK%2C_Germany_and_France.png/800px-Subsidy_per_passenger_journey_for_UK%2C_Germany_and_France.png 2x" data-file-width="810" data-file-height="488" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Subsidy_per_passenger_journey_for_UK,_Germany_and_France.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rail_subsidies" title="Rail subsidies">Rail subsidy</a> per passenger journey for the UK, Germany, France, Italy and Spain</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Rail_subsidies" title="Rail subsidies">Rail subsidies</a> have increased from \xc2\xa32.5bn in 1992-93 to \xc2\xa33.3bn in 2015-16 (in current prices), although subsidy per journey has fallen from \xc2\xa33.32 per journey to \xc2\xa31.89 per journey.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-orr.gov.uk_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-orr.gov.uk-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> However, this masks great regional variation, as in 2014-15 funding varied from "\xc2\xa31.41 per passenger journey in England to \xc2\xa36.51 per journey in Scotland and \xc2\xa38.34 per journey in Wales."<sup id="cite_ref-orr.gov.uk_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-orr.gov.uk-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rail_fatalities_of_European_countries.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Rail_fatalities_of_European_countries.png/300px-Rail_fatalities_of_European_countries.png" width="300" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Rail_fatalities_of_European_countries.png/450px-Rail_fatalities_of_European_countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Rail_fatalities_of_European_countries.png/600px-Rail_fatalities_of_European_countries.png 2x" data-file-width="736" data-file-height="451" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rail_fatalities_of_European_countries.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rail fatalities per billion passenger-km in European countries</div></div></div>\n<p>The public image of rail travel was severely damaged by a series of significant accidents after privatisation. These included the <a href="/wiki/Hatfield_rail_crash" title="Hatfield rail crash">Hatfield accident</a>, caused by a rail fragmenting due to the development of microscopic cracks. Following this, the rail infrastructure company Railtrack imposed over 1,200 emergency speed restrictions across its network and instigated an extremely costly nationwide track replacement programme. The consequent severe operational disruption to the national network and the company\'s spiralling costs set in motion <a href="/wiki/Tom_Winsor#Rail_Regulator_1999-2004" title="Tom Winsor">a series of events</a> which resulted in the collapse of the company and its replacement with <a href="/wiki/Network_Rail" title="Network Rail">Network Rail</a>, a state-owned,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> not-for-profit company. According to the <a href="/wiki/European_Railway_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="European Railway Agency">European Railway Agency</a>, in 2013 Britain had the safest railways in Europe based on the number of train safety incidents.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the end of September 2003, the first part of <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_1" title="High Speed 1">High Speed 1</a>, a high-speed link to the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Tunnel" title="Channel Tunnel">Channel Tunnel</a> and onward to <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, was completed, significantly adding to the rail infrastructure of the country. The rest of the link, from north Kent to <a href="/wiki/St_Pancras_railway_station" title="St Pancras railway station">St Pancras railway station</a> in London, opened in 2007. A major programme of remedial work on the <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_Main_Line" title="West Coast Main Line">West Coast Main Line</a> started in 1997 and finished in 2009.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In the 2010s, many upgrades are under way, such as the <a href="/wiki/Thameslink_Programme" title="Thameslink Programme">Thameslink Programme</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crossrail" title="Crossrail">Crossrail</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hub" title="Northern Hub">Northern Hub</a> and electrification of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Western_Main_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Western Main Line">Great Western Main Line</a>. Electrification plans for the <a href="/wiki/Midland_Main_Line" title="Midland Main Line">Midland Main Line</a> and the cross-Pennine line between Manchester and Leeds have been paused with the start of work on these projects postponed to some indefinite date(s)in the 2020s. Planning for <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_2" title="High Speed 2">High Speed 2</a> is underway, with a projected completion date of 2026 for Phase 1 (London to Birmingham) and 2033 for Phase 2.\n</p>\n<div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="History_of_rail_transport_in_Great_Britain" style="padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" #fdfdfd;padding:0px;text-align: center;;;;width:20%;"><div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/History_of_rail_transport_in_Great_Britain_1995_to_date" title="History of rail transport in Great Britain 1995 to date">1995 to date</a>\n<br /><i>Post-privatisation</i>\n</p>\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_rail_transport_in_Great_Britain_1995_to_date#Timeline_of_upgrades" title="History of rail transport in Great Britain 1995 to date">Timeline of future rail network upgrades in Great Britain</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_railway_lines_in_Great_Britain" title="List of railway lines in Great Britain">List of railway lines in Great Britain</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Passenger_services">Passenger services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Passenger services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_companies_operating_trains_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of companies operating trains in the United Kingdom">List of companies operating trains in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:222010_Attenborough_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/222010_Attenborough_%281%29.jpg/220px-222010_Attenborough_%281%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/222010_Attenborough_%281%29.jpg/330px-222010_Attenborough_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/222010_Attenborough_%281%29.jpg/440px-222010_Attenborough_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:222010_Attenborough_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_Trains" title="East Midlands Trains">East Midlands Trains</a> <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_222" title="British Rail Class 222">Class 222 Meridian</a> on a London to Nottingham service. These trains are used for InterCity services from London to the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands" title="East Midlands">East Midlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Yorkshire" title="South Yorkshire">South Yorkshire</a>.</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CrossCountry_170113_in_Snow.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/CrossCountry_170113_in_Snow.JPG/220px-CrossCountry_170113_in_Snow.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/CrossCountry_170113_in_Snow.JPG/330px-CrossCountry_170113_in_Snow.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/CrossCountry_170113_in_Snow.JPG/440px-CrossCountry_170113_in_Snow.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CrossCountry_170113_in_Snow.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/CrossCountry" title="CrossCountry">CrossCountry</a> <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_170" title="British Rail Class 170">Class 170</a> numbered 170113 with a service from <a href="/wiki/London_Stansted_Airport" title="London Stansted Airport">London Stansted Airport</a> to <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_New_Street_railway_station" title="Birmingham New Street railway station">Birmingham New Street</a></div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:365538_at_Cambridge_Rail_Station.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/365538_at_Cambridge_Rail_Station.JPG/220px-365538_at_Cambridge_Rail_Station.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/365538_at_Cambridge_Rail_Station.JPG/330px-365538_at_Cambridge_Rail_Station.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/365538_at_Cambridge_Rail_Station.JPG/440px-365538_at_Cambridge_Rail_Station.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:365538_at_Cambridge_Rail_Station.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/First_Capital_Connect" title="First Capital Connect">First Capital Connect</a> <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_365" title="British Rail Class 365">Class 365</a> \'Networker\' numbered 365538 at <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_railway_station" title="Cambridge railway station">Cambridge Rail Station</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Passenger services in Great Britain are divided into regional franchises and run by private (that is, non-state owned) <a href="/wiki/Train_operating_company" title="Train operating company">train operating companies</a>. These companies bid for seven- to eight-year contracts to run individual franchises. Most contracts are awarded by the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Transport" title="Department for Transport">Department for Transport</a> (DfT), with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Merseyrail" title="Merseyrail">Merseyrail</a>, where the franchise is awarded by <a href="/wiki/Merseyside_Passenger_Transport_Executive" class="mw-redirect" title="Merseyside Passenger Transport Executive">Merseyside Passenger Transport Executive</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ScotRail_(brand)" title="ScotRail (brand)">ScotRail</a>, where the DfT awards on the advice of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Government" title="Scottish Government">Scottish Government</a>. Initially, there were 25 franchises, but the number of different operating companies is smaller as some firms, including <a href="/wiki/FirstGroup" title="FirstGroup">FirstGroup</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stagecoach_Group" title="Stagecoach Group">Stagecoach Group</a>, run more than one franchise. In addition, some franchises have since been combined. There are also a number of local or specialised rail services operated on an \'open access\' basis outside the franchise arrangements. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Heathrow_Express" title="Heathrow Express">Heathrow Express</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hull_Trains" title="Hull Trains">Hull Trains</a>.\n</p><p>In the 2015&#8211;16 operating year, franchised services provided 1,718 million journeys totalling (64.7 billion billion passenger km) of travel, an increase over 1994&#8211;5 of 117% in journeys (from 761 million) and just over doubling the passenger miles.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The passenger-miles figure, after being flat from 1965 to 1995, surpassed the 1947 figure for the first time in 1998 and continues to rise steeply.\n</p><p>The key index used to assess passenger train performance is the <a href="/wiki/Public_Performance_Measure" title="Public Performance Measure">Public Performance Measure</a>, which combines figures for punctuality and reliability. From a base of 90% of trains arriving on time in 1998, the measure dipped to 75% in mid-2001 due to stringent safety restrictions put in place after the <a href="/wiki/Hatfield_rail_crash" title="Hatfield rail crash">Hatfield crash</a> in October 2000. However, in June 2015 the PPM stood at 91.2% after a period of steady increases in the annual moving average since 2003 until around 2012 when the improvements levelled off.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Train fares cost 2.7% more than under <a href="/wiki/British_Rail" title="British Rail">British Rail</a> in real terms on average.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> For some years, Britain has been said to have the highest rail fares in Europe, with peak-time and season tickets considerably higher than other countries, partly because <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_in_Europe#Subsidies" title="Rail transport in Europe">rail subsidies in Europe</a> are higher.<sup id="cite_ref-fullfact16082011_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fullfact16082011-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> However, passengers are also able to obtain some of the cheapest fares in Europe if they book in advance or travel at off-peak times<sup id="cite_ref-fullfact16082011_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fullfact16082011-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> or purchase \'day-return\' tickets which cost little more than a single ticket.\n</p><p>UK rail operators point out rail fare increases have been at a substantially lower rate than petrol prices for private motoring.<sup id="cite_ref-rail20110810p22_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rail20110810p22-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The difference in price has also been blamed on the fact Britain has the most restrictive <a href="/wiki/Loading_gauge" title="Loading gauge">loading gauge</a> (maximum width and height of trains that can fit through tunnels, bridges etc.) in the world which means any trains must be significantly narrower and less tall than those used elsewhere. This means British trains cannot be bought "off-the-shelf" and must be specially built to fit British standards.\n</p><p>Average rolling-stock age fell slightly from the third quarter of 2001&#8211;2 to the fourth quarter of 2013&#8211;4, from 20.7 years old to 19.4 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although passengers rarely have cause to refer to either document, all travel is subject to the <a href="/wiki/National_Rail_Conditions_of_Carriage" class="mw-redirect" title="National Rail Conditions of Carriage">National Rail Conditions of Carriage</a> and all tickets are valid subject to the rules set out in a number of so-called <a href="/wiki/UK_railway_technical_manuals" class="mw-redirect" title="UK railway technical manuals">technical manuals</a>, which are centrally produced for the network.\n</p>\n<div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="British_railway_ticketing" style="width:auto;clear:none;padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th scope="col" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Stevenage_Rail_Station.JPG/440px-Stevenage_Rail_Station.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stevenage_Rail_Station.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Stevenage_railway_station" title="Stevenage railway station">Stevenage railway station</a> along the <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_Main_Line" title="East Coast Main Line">East Coast Main Line</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:380004_at_Largs_2011.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/380004_at_Largs_2011.jpg/220px-380004_at_Largs_2011.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/380004_at_Largs_2011.jpg/440px-380004_at_Largs_2011.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div with a <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_380" title="British Rail Class 380">Class 380</a> run by <a href="/wiki/Abellio_ScotRail" title="Abellio ScotRail">Abellio ScotRail</a> a subsidiary of <a through TfL</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Railway_stations">Railway stations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Railway stations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Category:Railway_stations_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Category:Railway stations in the United Kingdom">Category:Railway stations in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:London_Victoria_Station_frontage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/London_Victoria_Station_frontage.jpg/220px-London_Victoria_Station_frontage.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/London_Victoria_Station_frontage.jpg/330px-London_Victoria_Station_frontage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/London_Victoria_Station_frontage.jpg/440px-London_Victoria_Station_frontage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:London_Victoria_Station_frontage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fa\xc3\xa7ade of <a href="/wiki/London_Victoria_station" title="London Victoria station">London Victoria</a></div></div></div>\n<p>There are 2,560 passenger <a href="/wiki/Railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Railway station">railway stations</a> on the Network Rail network.<sup id="cite_ref-ORR-infra-2016-17_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ORR-infra-2016-17-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ORR-table-2.53_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ORR-table-2.53-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> This does not include the <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a>, nor other systems which are not part of the national network, such as heritage railways. Most date from the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> and a number are in or on the edge of <a href="/wiki/Town_centre" title="Town centre">town</a> and <a href="/wiki/City_centre" title="City centre">city centres</a>. Major stations lie for the most part in large cities, with the largest conurbations (e.g. Liverpool, Birmingham, Bristol, Cardiff, Edinburgh, Glasgow and Manchester) typically having more than one main station. London is a major hub of the network, with 12 main-line termini forming a "ring" around <a href="/wiki/Central_London" title="Central London">central London</a>. Birmingham, Leeds, Manchester, Glasgow, Bristol and <a href="/wiki/Reading,_Berkshire" title="Reading, Berkshire">Reading</a> are major interchanges for many cross-country journeys that do not involve London. However, some important railway junction stations lie in smaller cities and towns, for example <a href="/wiki/York_railway_station" title="York railway station">York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crewe_railway_station" title="Crewe railway station">Crewe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ely_railway_station" title="Ely railway station">Ely</a>. Some other places expanded into towns and cities because of the railway network. <a href="/wiki/Swindon" title="Swindon">Swindon</a>, for example, was little more than a village before the <a href="/wiki/Great_Western_Railway" title="Great Western Railway">Great Western Railway</a> chose to site its locomotive works there. In many instances geography, politics or military considerations<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> originally caused stations to be sited further from the towns they served until, with time, these issues could be overcome (for example, <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth" title="Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a> had its original station at <a href="/wiki/Gosport" title="Gosport">Gosport</a>).\n</p>\n<div role="navigation" class="navbox" id="High-speed_rail">High-speed rail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: High-speed rail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_main_lines_railway_diagram.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/British_main_lines_railway_diagram.png/220px-British_main_lines_railway_diagram.png" width="220" height="313" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/British_main_lines_railway_diagram.png/330px-British_main_lines_railway_diagram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/British_main_lines_railway_diagram.png/440px-British_main_lines_railway_diagram.png 2x" data-file-width="2464" data-file-height="3508" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_main_lines_railway_diagram.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview map of the North-South main lines in the UK.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St_Pancras_railway_station_MMB_31_395018.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/St_Pancras_railway_station_MMB_31_395018.jpg/220px-St_Pancras_railway_station_MMB_31_395018.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/St_Pancras_railway_station_MMB_31_395018.jpg/330px-St_Pancras_railway_station_MMB_31_395018.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/St_Pancras_railway_station_MMB_31_395018.jpg/440px-St_Pancras_railway_station_MMB_31_395018.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2090" data-file-height="1345" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St_Pancras_railway_station_MMB_31_395018.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_(train_operating_company)" title="Southeastern (train operating company)">Southeastern</a> <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_395" title="British Rail Class 395">Class 395</a> at London <a href="/wiki/St_Pancras_railway_station" title="St Pancras railway station">St Pancras railway station</a></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="High-speed rail in the United Kingdom">High-speed rail in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>High-speed rail (above 124&#160;mph or 200&#160;km/h) was first introduced in Great Britain in the 1970s by British Rail. BR had pursued two development projects in parallel, the development of a <a href="/wiki/Tilting_train" title="Tilting train">tilting train</a> technology, the \'<a href="/wiki/Advanced_Passenger_Train" title="Advanced Passenger Train">Advanced Passenger Train</a>\' (APT), and development of a conventional high-speed diesel train, the \'High Speed Train\' (HST). The APT project was abandoned, but the HST design entered service as the <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Classes_253,_254_and_255" title="British Rail Classes 253, 254 and 255">British Rail Classes 253, 254 and 255</a> trains. The prototype HST, the <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_252" title="British Rail Class 252">British Rail Class 252</a>, reached a world speed record for diesel trains of 143.2&#160;mph, while the main fleet entered service limited to a service speed of 125&#160;mph, and were introduced progressively on main lines across the country, with a rebranding of their services as the <i><a href="/wiki/InterCity_125" title="InterCity 125">InterCity 125</a></i>. With electrification of the <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_Main_Line" title="East Coast Main Line">East Coast Main Line</a>, high-speed rail in Great Britain was augmented with the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_91" title="British Rail Class 91">British Rail Class 91</a>, intended for passenger service at up to 140&#160;mph (225&#160;km/h), and thus branded as the <i><a href="/wiki/InterCity_225" title="InterCity 225">InterCity 225</a></i>. The Class 91 units were designed for a maximum service speed of 140&#160;mph, and running at this speed was trialled with a \'flashing green\' signal aspect under the <a href="/wiki/UK_railway_signalling" title="UK railway signalling">British signalling system</a>. The trains were eventually limited to the same speed as the HST, to 125&#160;mph, with higher speeds deemed to require <a href="/wiki/Cab_signalling" title="Cab signalling">cab signalling</a>, which as of 2010 was not in place on the normal British railway network (but was used on the Channel Tunnel Rail Link). A final attempt by the nationalised British Rail at High Speed Rail was the cancelled <a href="/wiki/InterCity_250" title="InterCity 250">InterCity 250</a> project in the 1990s for the West Coast Main Line.\n</p><p>Post privatisation, a plan to upgrade the <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_Main_Line" title="West Coast Main Line">West Coast Main Line</a> to speeds of up to 140&#160;mph with infrastructure improvements were finally abandoned, although the tilting train <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_390" title="British Rail Class 390">Class 390</a> <i>Pendolino</i> fleet designed for this maximum speed of service were still built and entered service in 2002, and operates limited to 125&#160;mph. Other routes in the UK were upgraded with trains capable of top speeds of up to 125&#160;mph running with the introduction between 2000 and 2005 of <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_180" title="British Rail Class 180">Class 180</a> <i>Adelante</i> DMUs and the <a href="/wiki/Bombardier_Voyager_family" title="Bombardier Voyager family">Bombardier Voyager family</a> of DEMUs (Classes 220, 221 and 222).\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Speed_1">High Speed 1</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: High Speed 1">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/HS1" class="mw-redirect" title="HS1">HS1</a></div>\n<p>The first implementation of high-speed rail up to 186&#160;mph in regular passenger service in Great Britain was the Channel Tunnel Rail Link (now known as High Speed 1), when its first phase opened in 2003 linking the British end of the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Tunnel" title="Channel Tunnel">Channel Tunnel</a> at Folkestone with <a href="/wiki/Fawkham_Junction" title="Fawkham Junction">Fawkham Junction</a> in Kent. This is used by international only passenger trains for the <a href="/wiki/Eurostar" title="Eurostar">Eurostar</a> service, using <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_373" title="British Rail Class 373">British Rail Class 373</a> trains. The line was later extended all the way into <a href="/wiki/London_St_Pancras" class="mw-redirect" title="London St Pancras">London St Pancras</a> in 2007.\n</p><p>After the building of the first of a new <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_395" title="British Rail Class 395">British Rail Class 395</a> train fleet for use partly on High Speed 1 and parts of the rest of the UK rail network, the first domestic high-speed running over 125&#160;mph (to about 140&#160;mph) began in December 2009, including a special Olympic Javelin shuttle for the <a href="/wiki/2012_Olympics" class="mw-redirect" title="2012 Olympics">2012 Olympics</a>. These services are operated by the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_(train_operating_company)" title="Southeastern (train operating company)">Southeastern</a> franchise.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intercity_Express_Programme">Intercity Express Programme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Intercity Express Programme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Intercity_Express_Programme" title="Intercity Express Programme">Intercity Express Programme</a></div>\n<p>For replacement of the domestic fleet of Intercity 125 and 225 trains on the existing national network, the Intercity Express Programme was announced. In 2009 it was announced the preferred rolling stock option for this project was the <a href="/wiki/Hitachi_Super_Express" class="mw-redirect" title="Hitachi Super Express">Hitachi Super Express</a> family of multiple units, and they are expected to enter service from 2017 on the Great Western Main Line and 2018 on the East Coast Main Line. The trains will be capable of a maximum speed of 140&#160;mph with "minor modifications", with the necessary signalling modifications required of the <a href="/wiki/Network_Rail" title="Network Rail">Network Rail</a> infrastructure in Britain likely to come from the phased roll out of the Europe-wide <a href="/wiki/European_Rail_Traffic_Management_System" title="European Rail Traffic Management System">European Rail Traffic Management System</a> (ERTMS).\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Speed_2">High Speed 2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: High Speed 2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_2" title="High Speed 2">High Speed 2</a></div>\n<p>Following several studies and consultations on high-speed rail, in 2009 the UK Government formally announced the <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_2" title="High Speed 2">High Speed 2</a> project, establishing a company to produce a feasibility study to examine route options and financing for a new high-speed railway in the UK. This study began on the assumption the route would be a new purpose-built high-speed line connected to High-Speed 1 to the Channel tunnel and from London to the <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_(region)" title="West Midlands (region)">West Midlands</a>, via <a href="/wiki/London_Heathrow" class="mw-redirect" title="London Heathrow">London Heathrow</a>, relieving traffic on the <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_Main_Line" title="West Coast Main Line">West Coast Main Line</a>. Conventional high-speed rail technology would be used as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Maglev_(transport)" class="mw-redirect" title="Maglev (transport)">Maglev</a>. The rolling stock would be capable of travelling on the existing <a href="/wiki/Network_Rail" title="Network Rail">Network Rail</a> infrastructure if required, with the route intersecting with the existing West Coast Main Line (WCML) and the East Coast Main Line (ECML). A second phase of the project is planned to reach further north to Manchester, Sheffield and Leeds, as well as linking into the <a href="/wiki/Midland_Main_Line" title="Midland Main Line">Midland Main Line</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Speed_3">High Speed 3</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: High Speed 3">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_3" class="mw-redirect" title="High Speed 3">High Speed 3</a></div>\n<p>In June 2014, <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Osborne" title="George Osborne">George Osborne</a> proposed a high-speed rail link <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_3" class="mw-redirect" title="High Speed 3">High Speed 3</a> (HS3) between Liverpool and Newcastle/Sheffield/Hull. The line would utilise the existing route between Liverpool and Newcastle/Hull and a new route from to Sheffield will follow the same route to Manchester Victoria and then a new line from Victoria to Sheffield, with additional tunnels and other infrastructure.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High-speed_rolling_stock">High-speed rolling stock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: High-speed rolling stock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In August 2009 the speeds of the fastest trains operating in Great Britain capable of a top speed of over 125&#160;mph were as follows:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" cellpadding="5">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Name\n</th>\n<th data-sort-type="number">Locomotive class\n</th>\n<th>Type\n</th>\n<th href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_801" title="British Rail Class 801">801</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_802" title="British Rail Class 802">802</a>\n</td>\n<td>EMU\n</td>\n<td>???\n</td>\n<td>140 (225)\n</td>\n<td>125 (200)\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In 2011 the fastest timetabled start-to-stop run by a UK domestic train service was the Hull Trains 07.30 King\'s Cross to Hull, which covered the 125.4&#160;km (78 miles) from Stevenage to Grantham in 42 minutes at an average speed of 179.1&#160;km/h (111.4&#160;mph). This is operated by a <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_180" title="British Rail Class 180">Class 180</a> diesel unit running "under the wires" on this East Coast route. This was matched by several Leeds to London <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_91" title="British Rail Class 91">Class 91</a>-operated <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_(train_operating_company)" title="East Coast (train operating company)">East Coast</a> trains if their two-minute recovery allowance for this section is excluded from the public timetable.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_metro_and_other_rail_systems">Local metro and other rail systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Local metro and other rail systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="floatright"><a href="/wiki/File:Urban_rail_in_the_UK.svg" class="image"><img alt="Urban rail in the UK.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Urban_rail_in_the_UK.svg/200px-Urban_rail_in_the_UK.svg.png" width="200" height="365" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Urban_rail_in_the_UK.svg/300px-Urban_rail_in_the_UK.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Urban_rail_in_the_UK.svg/400px-Urban_rail_in_the_UK.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1064" data-file-height="1942" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LUL-S-Stock-arrives-Croxley.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/LUL-S-Stock-arrives-Croxley.jpg/220px-LUL-S-Stock-arrives-Croxley.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/LUL-S-Stock-arrives-Croxley.jpg/330px-LUL-S-Stock-arrives-Croxley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/LUL-S-Stock-arrives-Croxley.jpg/440px-LUL-S-Stock-arrives-Croxley.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LUL-S-Stock-arrives-Croxley.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a> <a href="/wiki/London_Underground_S7_and_S8_Stock" title="London Underground S7 and S8 Stock">S-stock</a> train</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Rapid transit in the United Kingdom">Rapid transit in the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commuter_rail_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Commuter rail in the United Kingdom">Commuter rail in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>A number of towns and cities have <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">rapid transit</a> systems. Heavy rail underground technology is used in the <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_subway" class="mw-redirect" title="Glasgow subway">Glasgow</a> Underground systems while the <a href="/wiki/Merseyrail" title="Merseyrail">Merseyrail</a> system in <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> shares some characteristics. Light rail with some underground sections exist in <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Tyne_and_Wear_Metro" title="Tyne and Wear Metro">Tyne and Wear Metro</a>  and in the <a href="/wiki/Docklands_Light_Railway" title="Docklands Light Railway">London Docklands</a>. The light rail systems in <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Express_Transit" title="Nottingham Express Transit">Nottingham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Supertram" title="Sheffield Supertram">Sheffield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Metrolink" title="Manchester Metrolink">Manchester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tramlink" title="Tramlink">Croydon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Midland_Metro" class="mw-redirect" title="Midland Metro">Birmingham/Black Country</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Trams" title="Edinburgh Trams">Edinburgh</a> use a combination of street running tramways and, where available, reserved right of way or former conventional rail lines in some suburbs. <a href="/wiki/Blackpool_tramway" class="mw-redirect" title="Blackpool tramway">Blackpool</a> has the one remaining traditional tram system. Monorails, heritage tramways, miniature railways and funiculars also exist in several places. In addition, there are a number of heritage (mainly steam) standard and narrow gauge railways, and a few industrial railways and tramways. Some lines which appear to be heritage operations sometimes claim to be part of the public transport network; the <a href="/wiki/Romney,_Hythe_and_Dymchurch_Railway" title="Romney, Hythe and Dymchurch Railway">Romney, Hythe and Dymchurch Railway</a> in Kent regularly transports schoolchildren.\n</p><p>Most major cities have some form of <a href="/wiki/Commuter_rail_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Commuter rail in the United Kingdom">commuter rail network</a>. These include <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>, Liverpool, London and <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>.\n</p>\n<dl><dd></dd></dl>\n<div role="navigation" class="navbox"
