Kingdom">London</a></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Goods_services">Goods services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Goods services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rail_freight_transport_in_Great_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Rail freight transport in Great Britain">Rail freight transport in Great Britain</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:66583_Chesterfield.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/66583_Chesterfield.jpg/220px-66583_Chesterfield.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/66583_Chesterfield.jpg/330px-66583_Chesterfield.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/66583_Chesterfield.jpg/440px-66583_Chesterfield.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3078" data-file-height="2052" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:66583_Chesterfield.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Freightliner_Group" title="Freightliner Group">Freightliner</a> <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_66" title="British Rail Class 66">Class 66</a> pulling coal at <a href="/wiki/Chesterfield" title="Chesterfield">Chesterfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire" title="Derbyshire">Derbyshire</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rail_freight_in_the_UK_1997-2016.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Rail_freight_in_the_UK_1997-2016.png/250px-Rail_freight_in_the_UK_1997-2016.png" width="250" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Rail_freight_in_the_UK_1997-2016.png/375px-Rail_freight_in_the_UK_1997-2016.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Rail_freight_in_the_UK_1997-2016.png/500px-Rail_freight_in_the_UK_1997-2016.png 2x" data-file-width="1003" data-file-height="658" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rail_freight_in_the_UK_1997-2016.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Million tonnes of rail freight moved in the UK from 1983 to 2018 (annual rolling average). There was a large decrease in coal carried in 1984-5 due to the <a href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984%E2%80%9385)" title="UK miners&#39; strike (1984\xe2\x80\x9385)">miners\' strike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>There are four main <a href="/wiki/Freight_rail_transport" class="mw-redirect" title="Freight rail transport">goods</a> operating companies in the UK, the largest of which is <a href="/wiki/DB_Cargo_UK" title="DB Cargo UK">DB Cargo UK</a> (formerly DB Schenker formerly English Welsh &amp; Scottish (EWS)). There are also several smaller independent operators including <a href="/wiki/Mendip_Rail" title="Mendip Rail">Mendip Rail</a>. Types of freight carried include <i><a href="/wiki/Intermodal_freight_transport" title="Intermodal freight transport">intermodal</a></i> &#8212; in essence containerised freight &#8212; and coal, metals, oil, and construction material. The Beeching Cuts, in contrast to passenger services, greatly modernised the goods sector, replacing inefficient wagons with containerised regional hubs.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Freight services had been in steady decline since the 1930s, initially because of the loss of manufacturing and then road haulage\'s cost advantage in combination with higher wages.<sup id="cite_ref-amazonaws92119643_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amazonaws92119643-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Since 1995, however, the amount of <a href="/wiki/Freight" class="mw-redirect" title="Freight">freight</a> carried on the railways has increased sharply due to increased reliability and competition, as well as international services.<sup id="cite_ref-amazonaws92119643_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amazonaws92119643-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-unece_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unece-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Transport" title="Department for Transport">Department for Transport</a>\'s <i>Transport Ten Year Plan</i> calls for an 80% increase in rail freight measured from a 2000&#8211;1 base.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Statistics on freight are specified in terms of the weight of freight lifted, and the <i>net tonne kilometre</i>, being freight weight multiplied by distance carried. 116.6 million tonnes of freight was lifted in the 2013&#8211;4 period, against 138 million tonnes in 1986&#8211;7, a decrease of 16%.<sup id="cite_ref-gov12189_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gov12189-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> However, a record 22.7 billion net tonne kilometres (14 billion net ton miles) of freight movement were recorded in 2013-4, against 16.6 billion (10.1 billion) in 1986&#8211;7, an increase of 38%.<sup id="cite_ref-gov12189_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gov12189-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Coal makes up 36% of the total <i>net tonne kilometre</i>, though its share is declining.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Rail freight has increased its market share since privatisation (by net tonne kilometres) from 7.4% in 1998 to 11.1% in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Recent growth is partly due to more international services including the Channel Tunnel and <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Felixstowe" title="Port of Felixstowe">Port of Felixstowe</a>, which is containerised.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, network bottlenecks and insufficient investment in catering for 9\' 6" high shipping containers restrict growth.<sup id="cite_ref-unece_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unece-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A symbolic loss to the rail freight industry in Great Britain was the custom of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a>, which from 2004 discontinued use of its 49-train fleet, and switching to road haulage after a near 170-year-preference for trains. <a href="/wiki/Travelling_Post_Office" title="Travelling Post Office">Mail trains</a> had long been part of the tradition of the railways in Great Britain, famously celebrated in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Night_Mail" title="Night Mail">Night Mail</a></i>, for which <a href="/wiki/W._H._Auden" title="W. H. Auden">W. H. Auden</a> wrote the poem of the same name. Although Royal Mail suspended the <a href="/wiki/Travelling_Post_Office" title="Travelling Post Office">Mail train</a> in January 2004, this decision was reversed in December of the same year, and <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_325" title="British Rail Class 325">Class 325s</a> are now used on some routes including between London, Warrington and Scotland.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Train_leasing_services">Train leasing services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Train leasing services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><span id="Leasing_services"></span>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Class_47_railway_locomotive_-_Fragonset_Black_livery_-_Virginia_Water_railway_station_-_280404.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Class_47_railway_locomotive_-_Fragonset_Black_livery_-_Virginia_Water_railway_station_-_280404.jpg/220px-Class_47_railway_locomotive_-_Fragonset_Black_livery_-_Virginia_Water_railway_station_-_280404.jpg" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Class_47_railway_locomotive_-_Fragonset_Black_livery_-_Virginia_Water_railway_station_-_280404.jpg/330px-Class_47_railway_locomotive_-_Fragonset_Black_livery_-_Virginia_Water_railway_station_-_280404.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Class_47_railway_locomotive_-_Fragonset_Black_livery_-_Virginia_Water_railway_station_-_280404.jpg/440px-Class_47_railway_locomotive_-_Fragonset_Black_livery_-_Virginia_Water_railway_station_-_280404.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="390" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Class_47_railway_locomotive_-_Fragonset_Black_livery_-_Virginia_Water_railway_station_-_280404.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fragonset Railways class 47 railway locomotive, Virginia Water railway station, April 2004</div></div></div>\n<p>At the time of privatisation, the rolling stock of <a href="/wiki/British_Rail" title="British Rail">British Rail</a> was sold to the new operators, as in the case of the freight companies, or to the three ROSCOs (<a href="/wiki/Rolling_stock_operating_company" title="Rolling stock operating company">rolling stock operating companies</a>) which lease or hire stock to passenger and freight train operators. Leasing is relatively commonplace in transport, since it enables operating companies to avoid the complication associated with raising sufficient capital to purchase assets; instead, assets are leased and paid for from ongoing revenue. Since 1994 there has been a growth in smaller spot-hire companies that provide rolling stock on short-term contracts. Many of these have grown thanks to the major selling-off of locomotives by the large freight operators, especially EWS.\n</p><p>Unlike other major players in the privatised railway system of Great Britain, the ROSCOs are not subject to close regulation by the economic regulatory authority. They were expected to compete with one another, and they do, although not in all respects.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition_codes_of_practice">Competition codes of practice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Competition codes of practice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since privatisation in 1995, the ROSCOs have faced criticism from several quarters - including passenger train operating companies such as GNER, Arriva and FirstGroup - on the basis they are acting as an <a href="/wiki/Oligopoly" title="Oligopoly">oligopoly</a> to keep lease prices higher than they would be in a competitive market. In 1998, <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister" class="mw-redirect" title="Deputy Prime Minister">Deputy Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Prescott" title="John Prescott">John Prescott</a> asked <a href="/wiki/Rail_Regulator" title="Rail Regulator">Rail Regulator</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Swift_QC" class="mw-redirect" title="John Swift QC">John Swift QC</a> to investigate the market\'s operation and make recommendations. Many believed Prescott favoured much closer regulation of the ROSCOs, perhaps bringing them into the net of contract-specific regulation, i.e., requiring every rolling stock lease to be approved by the Rail Regulator before it could be valid. Swift\'s report did not find major problems with the operation of what was then an infant market, and instead recommended the ROSCOs sign up to voluntary, non-binding codes of practice in relation to their future behaviour. Prescott did not like this, but he did not have the legislative time allocation to do much about it. Swift\'s successor as Rail Regulator, <a href="/wiki/Tom_Winsor" title="Tom Winsor">Tom Winsor</a>, agreed with Swift and the ROSCOs were happy to go along with codes of practice, coupled with the Rail Regulator\'s new powers to deal with abuse of dominance and anti-competitive behaviour under the <a href="/wiki/Competition_Act_1998" title="Competition Act 1998">Competition Act 1998</a>. In establishing these codes, the Rail Regulator made it clear he expected the ROSCOs to adhere to their letter and spirit. The codes of practice were duly put in place and for the next five years the Rail Regulator received no complaints about ROSCO behaviour.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="White_paper_2004">White paper 2004</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: White paper 2004">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Virgin_Pendolino_at_Euston.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Virgin_Pendolino_at_Euston.jpg/220px-Virgin_Pendolino_at_Euston.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Virgin_Pendolino_at_Euston.jpg/330px-Virgin_Pendolino_at_Euston.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Virgin_Pendolino_at_Euston.jpg/440px-Virgin_Pendolino_at_Euston.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="532" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Virgin_Pendolino_at_Euston.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Virgin Pendolino Train</div></div></div>\n<p>In July 2004, the Department for Transport\'s White Paper on the future of the railways contained a statement it was dissatisfied with the operation of the rolling stock leasing market and believed there may have been excessive pricing on the part of the ROSCOs.\n</p><p>In June 2006, <a href="/wiki/Gwyneth_Dunwoody" title="Gwyneth Dunwoody">Gwyneth Dunwoody</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="British House of Commons">House of Commons</a> <a href="/wiki/Transport_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="Transport Committee">Transport Committee</a> chair, called for an investigation into the companies.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Transport commentator <a href="/wiki/Christian_Wolmar" title="Christian Wolmar">Christian Wolmar</a> has asserted the high cost of leasing is due to the way the franchises are distributed to the train operating companies. While the TOCs are negotiating for a franchise they have some freedom to propose different rolling stock options. It is only once they have won the franchise, however, they start negotiating with the ROSCOs. The ROSCO will know the TOC\'s requirements and also knows the TOC has to obtain a fixed mix of rolling stock which puts the train operating company at a disadvantage in its negotiations with the ROSCO. However, Wolmar considers it a mistake to blame the ROSCOs who are simply behaving as commercial companies always behave. Ultimately the problem for Wolmar is the system \xe2\x80\x93 and that is down to the government, who he believes are not prepared to seek a more workable solution.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition_Commission">Competition Commission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Competition Commission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 29 November 2006, following a June 2006 complaint by the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Transport" title="Department for Transport">Department for Transport</a> alleging excessive pricing by the ROSCOs, the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Rail_and_Road" title="Office of Rail and Road">Office of Rail Regulation</a> (as it was then called) announced it was minded to refer the operation of the market for passenger rolling stock to the <a href="/wiki/Competition_Commission" title="Competition Commission">Competition Commission</a>, citing, amongst other factors, problems in the DfT\'s own franchising policy as responsible for what may be regarded as a dysfunctional market. ORR said it will consult the industry and the public on what to do, and will publish its decision in April 2007. If the ORR does refer the market to the Competition Commission, there may well be a hiatus in investment in new rolling stock whilst the ROSCOs and their parent companies wait to hear what return they will be allowed to make on their train fleets. This could have the <a href="/wiki/Unintended_consequence" class="mw-redirect" title="Unintended consequence">unintended consequence</a> of intensifying the problem of overcrowding on some routes because TOCs will be unable to lengthen their trains or acquire new ones if they need the ROSCOs to co-operate in their acquisition or financing. Some commentators have suggested that such an outcome would be detrimental to the public interest. This is especially striking since the <a href="/wiki/National_Audit_Office_(United_Kingdom)" title="National Audit Office (United Kingdom)">National Audit Office</a>, in its November 2006 report on the renewal and upgrade of the <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_main_line" class="mw-redirect" title="West Coast main line">West Coast main line</a>, said that the capacity of the trains and the network will be full in the next few years and advocated train lengthening as an important measure to cope with sharply higher passenger numbers.\n</p><p>The Competition Commission conducted an investigation and published provisional findings<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> on 7 August 2008. The report was published on 7 April 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> A press release\n<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> summarised the recommendations as follows:\n</p>\n<ul><li>introduce longer franchise terms (in the region of 12 to 15 years or longer), which would allow TOCs to realise the benefits and recover the costs of switching to alternative new or used rolling stock over a longer period, which should increase the incentives and ability for TOCs to exercise choice</li>\n<li>assess the benefits of alternative new or used rolling stock proposals beyond the franchise term and across other franchises when evaluating franchise bids. This will encourage a wider choice of rolling stock to be considered in franchise proposals, irrespective of franchise length</li>\n<li>ensure franchise invitations to tender (ITTs) are specified in such a way franchise bidders are allowed a choice of rolling stock</li>\n<li>requiring the ROSCOs to remove non-discrimination requirements from the Codes of Practice, which would provide greater incentives for the TOCs to seek improved terms from the ROSCOs</li>\n<li>requiring rolling stock lessors to provide TOCs with a set list of information when making a lease rental offer for used rolling stock, which would give TOCs the ability to negotiate more effectively</li></ul>\n<h3><span id="Leasing_companies_.28ROSCO.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Leasing_companies_(ROSCO)">Leasing companies (ROSCO)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Leasing companies (ROSCO)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<dl><dd><i>See also <a href="/wiki/Rolling_stock_operating_company" title="Rolling stock operating company">Rolling stock operating company</a></i></dd></dl>\n<p>Three companies took over <a href="/wiki/British_Rail" title="British Rail">British Rail</a>\'s rolling stock on privatisation:\n</p>\n<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Angel_Trains" title="Angel Trains">Angel Trains</a></b> - owned by a consortium of private equity investors, mainly comprising pension funds and insurance companies, and has 4,400 vehicles in the UK.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Eversholt_Rail_Group" title="Eversholt Rail Group">Eversholt Rail Group</a></b> - owns a fleet of over 4,000 vehicles and is owned by a consortium including <a href="/wiki/3i_Infrastructure" title="3i Infrastructure">3i Infrastructure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a>.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Porterbrook" title="Porterbrook">Porterbrook</a></b> - leases some 3,500 locomotives, trains and freight wagons; owned by a consortium including <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lloyds_TSB" class="mw-redirect" title="Lloyds TSB">Lloyds TSB</a> (who withdrew in October 2010) and <a href="/wiki/BNP_Paribas" title="BNP Paribas">BNP Paribas</a>.</li></ul>\n<p>A number of other companies have since entered the leasing market:\n</p>\n<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Sovereign_Trains" title="Sovereign Trains">Sovereign Trains</a></b> - a company that forms part of the same group as the open-access operator <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_(train_operating_company)" title="Grand Central (train operating company)">Grand Central</a>. Sovereign Trains owned the rolling stock operated by Grand Central.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/QW_Rail_Leasing" title="QW Rail Leasing">QW Rail Leasing</a></b> - a joint venture between the <a href="/wiki/National_Australia_Bank" title="National Australia Bank">National Australia Bank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sumitomo_Mitsui_Banking_Corporation" title="Sumitomo Mitsui Banking Corporation">SMBC Leasing and Finance</a> to provide the EMU rolling stock to <a href="/wiki/London_Overground" title="London Overground">London Overground</a>.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Diesel_Trains_Ltd" title="Diesel Trains Ltd">Diesel Trains</a></b> - in March 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Transport" title="Department for Transport">Department for Transport</a> also launched its own ROSCO to order 202 new diesel train carriages for the Thames Valley area, around Bristol and on longer distances in northern England. The trains were due to enter service by 2012 for train operators <a href="/wiki/First_Great_Western" class="mw-redirect" title="First Great Western">First Great Western</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_TransPennine_Express" title="First TransPennine Express">First TransPennine Express</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rail" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Rail">Northern Rail</a>. However, in August 2009 the order was cancelled due to the planned electrification of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Western_Main_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Western Main Line">Great Western Main Line</a> and Diesel Trains was later dissolved in July 2012.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Lloyds_TSB_General_Leasing" class="mw-redirect" title="Lloyds TSB General Leasing">Lloyds TSB General Leasing</a></b> - in April 2009, <a href="/wiki/Lloyds_TSB" class="mw-redirect" title="Lloyds TSB">Lloyds TSB</a> entered the rolling stock market by funding the purchase of 30 new EMU trains for <a href="/wiki/National_Express_East_Anglia" title="National Express East Anglia">National Express East Anglia</a>.</li>\n<li><b>Beacon Rail Leasing</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> owns <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_68" title="British Rail Class 68">Class 68</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_88" title="British Rail Class 88">Class 88</a> locomotives, as well as <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_220" title="British Rail Class 220">Class 220</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_221" title="British Rail Class 221">Class 221</a> DMUs.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/UK_Rail_Leasing" title="UK Rail Leasing">UK Rail Leasing</a>, owns some <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_56" title="British Rail Class 56">Class 56</a> locomotives</li></ul>\n<div role="navigation" class="navbox" European Rail">Macquarie European Rail</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Porterbrook" title="Porterbrook">Porterbrook</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/QW_Rail_Leasing" title="QW Rail Leasing">QW Rail Leasing</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sovereign_Trains" title="Sovereign Trains">Sovereign Trains</a></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spot-hire_companies">Spot-hire companies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Spot-hire companies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Spot-hire companies provide short-term leasing of rolling stock.\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>MiddlePeak Railways</b>, a locomotive hire &amp; lease company with a stock of locomotives similar to <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_08" title="British Rail Class 08">Class 08</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Nederlandse_Spoorwegen" title="Nederlandse Spoorwegen">NS</a> 0-6-0 <a href="/wiki/NS_600_Class" class="mw-redirect" title="NS 600 Class">600 Class</a> shunting locomotives, other locomotives, rolling stock &amp; parts.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><b><a href="/w/index.php?title=GL_Railease&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="GL Railease (page does not exist)">GL Railease</a></b> owned by <a href="/wiki/GATX" title="GATX">GATX</a> This company works closely with <a href="/wiki/DB_Cargo_UK" title="DB Cargo UK">DB Cargo UK</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><b><a <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_57" title="British Rail Class 57">Class 57</a> locomotive.</li></ul>\n<div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="British_railway_spot-hire_companies" Rail</a></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Statutory_framework">Statutory framework</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Statutory framework">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_rail_industry_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Structure of the rail industry in the United Kingdom">Structure of the rail industry in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>Railways in Great Britain are in the private sector, but they are subject to control by central government, and to economic and safety regulation by arms of government.\n</p><p>In 2006, using powers in the <a href="/wiki/Railways_Act_2005" title="Railways Act 2005">Railways Act 2005</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Transport" title="Department for Transport">Department for Transport</a> took over most of the functions of the now wound up <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Rail_Authority" title="Strategic Rail Authority">Strategic Rail Authority</a>. The DfT now itself runs competitions for the award of passenger rail franchises, and, once awarded, monitors and enforces the contracts with the private sector franchisees. Franchises specify the passenger rail services which are to be run and the quality and other conditions (for example, the cleanliness of trains, station facilities and opening hours, the punctuality and reliability of trains) which the operators have to meet. Some franchises receive a subsidy from the DfT for doing so, and some are cash-positive, which means the franchisee pays the DfT for the contract. Some franchises start life as subsidised and, over their life, move to being cash-positive.\n</p><p>The other regulatory authority for the privatised railway is the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Rail_and_Road" title="Office of Rail and Road">Office of Rail and Road</a> (previously the Office of Rail Regulation), which, following the Railways Act 2005, is the combined economic and safety regulator. It replaced the <a href="/wiki/Rail_Regulator" title="Rail Regulator">Rail Regulator</a> on 5 July 2004. The <a href="/wiki/Rail_Safety_and_Standards_Board" title="Rail Safety and Standards Board">Rail Safety and Standards Board</a> still exists, however; established in 2003 on the recommendations of a public inquiry, it leads the industry\'s progress in health and safety matters.\n</p><p>The principal modern railway statutes are:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Railways_Act_1993" title="Railways Act 1993">Railways Act 1993</a><b></b></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Competition_Act_1998" title="Competition Act 1998">Competition Act 1998</a> (insofar as it confers competition powers on the Office of Rail and Road)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Transport_Act_2000" title="Transport Act 2000">Transport Act 2000</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Railways_and_Transport_Safety_Act_2003" title="Railways and Transport Safety Act 2003">Railways and Transport Safety Act 2003</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Railways_Act_2005" title="Railways Act 2005">Railways Act 2005</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Railway_industry">Railway industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Railway industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statutory_authorities">Statutory authorities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span (VSOE) \xe2\x80\x93 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.belmond.com/venice-simplon-orient-express/">Website</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span id="Early_railway_companies_.281820s-1840s.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_railway_companies_(1820s-1840s)">Early railway companies (1820s-1840s)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Early railway companies (1820s-1840s)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>This is only the earliest of the main line openings: for a more comprehensive list of the hundreds of early railways see <a href="/wiki/List_of_early_British_railway_companies" title="List of early British railway companies">List of early British railway companies</a>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Stockton_and_Darlington_Railway" title="Stockton and Darlington Railway">Stockton and Darlington Railway</a> (1825) - First steam-hauled passenger railway in the world.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Canterbury_and_Whitstable_Railway" title="Canterbury and Whitstable Railway">Canterbury and Whitstable Railway</a> (1830) - First steam-hauled passenger railway to issue season tickets.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liverpool_and_Manchester_Railway" title="Liverpool and Manchester Railway">Liverpool and Manchester Railway</a> (1830) - First InterCity passenger railway.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Grand_Junction_Railway" title="Grand Junction Railway">Grand Junction Railway</a> (1833) - The line built by the company was the first trunk railway to be completed in England, and arguably the world\'s first long-distance railway with steam traction.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/London_and_Greenwich_Railway" title="London and Greenwich Railway">London and Greenwich Railway</a> (1836) - First steam railway in the capital, the first to be built specifically for passengers, and the first elevated railway.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/London_and_Birmingham_Railway" title="London and Birmingham Railway">London and Birmingham Railway</a> (1837) -  First Intercity line to be built into London.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Midland_Counties_Railway" title="Midland Counties Railway">Midland Counties Railway</a> (1839)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Birmingham_and_Derby_Junction_Railway" title="Birmingham and Derby Junction Railway">Birmingham and Derby Junction Railway</a> (BDJR) (1839)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/North_Midland_Railway" title="North Midland Railway">North Midland Railway</a> (1840)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Taff_Vale_Railway" title="Taff Vale Railway">Taff Vale Railway</a> (1840)</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Heritage_and_private_railways">Heritage and private railways</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Heritage and private railways">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_heritage_and_private_railways" title="List of British heritage and private railways">List of British heritage and private railways</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Locomotive70013OliverCromwellNNR11March2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Locomotive70013OliverCromwellNNR11March2010.jpg/220px-Locomotive70013OliverCromwellNNR11March2010.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Locomotive70013OliverCromwellNNR11March2010.jpg/330px-Locomotive70013OliverCromwellNNR11March2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Locomotive70013OliverCromwellNNR11March2010.jpg/440px-Locomotive70013OliverCromwellNNR11March2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1330" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Locomotive70013OliverCromwellNNR11March2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Heritage railways are popular tourist attractions. The photo shows a preserved <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">locomotive</a> (<a href="/wiki/British_Railways" class="mw-redirect" title="British Railways">BR</a> Standard <a href="/wiki/BR_standard_class_7_70013_Oliver_Cromwell" class="mw-redirect" title="BR standard class 7 70013 Oliver Cromwell">7MT 70013 <i>Oliver Cromwell</i></a>) on the <a href="/wiki/North_Norfolk_Railway" title="North Norfolk Railway">North Norfolk Railway</a> on 11 March 2010.</div></div></div>\n<p>Many lines closed by British Railways, including many closed during the <a href="/wiki/Beeching_cuts" title="Beeching cuts">Beeching cuts</a>, have been restored and reopened as <a href="/wiki/Heritage_railway" title="Heritage railway">heritage railways</a>. A few have been relaid as narrow-gauge but the majority are standard-gauge. Most use both steam and diesel locomotives for haulage. Most heritage railways are operated as tourist attractions and do not provide regular year-round train services.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Railway_re-opening">Railway re-opening</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rail_transport_in_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Railway re-opening">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Several pressure groups are campaigning for the re-opening of closed railway lines in Great Britain. These include:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Ashington\xe2\x80\x93Bedlington\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Newcastle_railway_station" title="Newcastle railway station">Newcastle</a><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Marlow_Branch_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Marlow Branch Line">Marlow Branch</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bourne_End_railway_station" title="Bourne End railway station">Bourne End</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/High_Wycombe_railway_station" title="High Wycombe railway station">High Wycombe</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cambridge_railway_station" title="Cambridge railway station">Cambridge</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Oxford_railway_station" title="Oxford railway station">Oxford</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_West_Rail" title="East West Rail">East West Rail</a><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> This project was approved by the Government in November 2011.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Colne_railway_station" title="Colne railway station">Colne</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Skipton_railway_station" title="Skipton railway station">Skipton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skipton-East_Lancashire_Rail_Action_Partnership" class="mw-redirect" title="Skipton-East Lancashire Rail Action Partnership">Skipton-East Lancashire Rail Action Partnership</a><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/South_Staffordshire_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="South Staffordshire Line">South Staffordshire Line</a> (<a href="/wiki/Stourbridge_Junction_railway_station" title="Stourbridge Junction railway station">Stourbridge</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Walsall_railway_station" title="Walsall railway station">Walsall</a>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Wealden_Line" title="Wealden Line">Wealden Line</a> (<a href="/wiki/Uckfield_railway_station" title="Uckfield railway station">Uckfield</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Lewes_railway_station" title="Lewes railway station">Lewes</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Woodhead_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Woodhead Line">Woodhead Line</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hadfield_railway_station" title="Hadfield railway station">Hadfield</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Penistone_railway_station" title="Penistone railway station">Penistone</a>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/York_to_Beverley_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="York to Beverley Line">York to Beverley Line</a> (<a href="/wiki/York_railway_station" title="York railway station">York</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Beverley_railway_station" title="Beverley railway station">Beverley</a>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Peak_Rail" title="Peak Rail">Peak Rail</a>: (<a href="/wiki/Matlock_railway_station" title="Matlock railway station">Matlock</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Bakewell_railway_station" title="Bakewell railway station">Bakewell</a>). Under-funded line.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Great_Central_Main_Line" title="Great Central Main Line">Great Central Railway</a> Notts\xe2\x80\x93Leicester</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Portishead_Railway" title="Portishead Railway">Portishead Railway</a> from <a href="/wiki/Portishead,_Somerset" title="Portishead, Somerset">Portishead</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Temple_Meads_railway_station" title="Bristol Temple Meads railway station">Bristol Temple Meads</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/St_Andrews_Rail_Link" title="St Andrews Rail Link">St Andrews Rail Link</a> (<a href="/wiki/Leuchars_railway_station" title="Leuchars railway station">Leuchars</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/St_Andrews" title="St Andrews">St Andrews</a>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Carmarthen-Aberystwyth_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Carmarthen-Aberystwyth line">Carmarthen-Aberystwyth line</a></li></ul>\n<p>From 1995 until 2009, 27 new lines (totalling 199 track miles) and 68 stations were opened, with 65 further new station sites identified by <a href="/wiki/Network_Rail" title="Network Rail">Network Rail</a> or government for possible construction.<sup id="cite_ref-ATOCConnectingCommunitiesReportS106_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATOCConnectingCommunitiesReportS106-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\nOn 15 June 2009 the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Train_Operating_Companies" class="mw-redirect" title="Association of Train Operating Companies">Association of Train Operating Companies</a> (ATOC) published the report <i><a href="/wiki/Connecting_Communities:_Expanding_Access_to_the_Rail_Network" title="Connecting Communities: Expanding Access to the Rail Network">Connecting Communities: Expanding Access to the Rail Network</a></i>, detailing schemes around England where it believed there was a commercial business case for passenger network expansion. The published proposals involved the re-opening or new construction of 40 stations, serving communities with populations of over 15,000, including 14 schemes involving the re-opening or reconstruction of rail lines for passenger services. These would be short-lead-time local projects, to be completed in timescales ranging from 2 years 9 months to 6 years, once approved by <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_England" title="Local government in England">local</a> and <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_England" title="Regions of England">regional</a> governments, Network Rail and the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Transport" title="Department for Transport">Department for Transport</a>, complementing existing long-term national projects.<sup id="cite_ref-ATOCConnectingCommunitiesReportS10_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATOCConnectingCommunitiesReportS10-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC8099912_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC8099912-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Rail_link.28s.29_with_adjacent_countries"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rail_link(s)_with_adjacent_countries">Rail
